In the House, then-Representative Charles Schumer introduced a bill that made a version of that test applicable to all actions taken by the Federal Government or the States.  H.&nbsp;R. 1308, 103d Cong., 1st Sess. (1993).  This bill, which eventually became the Religious Freedom Restoration Act (RFRA), passed in the House without dissent, was approved in the Senate by a vote of 97 to 3, and was enthusiastically signed into law by President Clinton.  139 Cong. Rec. 27239-27341 (1993) (House voice vote); <em>id</em>., at 26416 (Senate vote); Remarks on Signing the Religious Freedom Restoration Act of 1993, 29 Weekly Comp. of Pres. Doc. 2377 (1993).  And when this Court later held in <em>City of Boerne</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 507, that Congress lacked the power under the 14th Amendment to impose these rules on the States, Congress responded by enacting the Religious Land Use and Institutionalized Persons Act (RLUIPA) under its spending power and its power to regulate interstate commerce.  See 114 Stat. 803.  Introduced in the Senate by Sen. Orrin Hatch and cosponsored by Sen. Edward Kennedy, RLUIPA imposed the same rules as RFRA on land use and prison regulations.  S. 2869, 106th Cong., 2d Sess. (2000); 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 2000cc <em>et seq</em>; 146 Cong. Rec. 16698 (2000).  RLUIPA passed both Houses of Congress without a single negative vote and, like RFRA, was signed by President Clinton.  <em>Id</em>., at 16703, 16623; Statement on Signing the Religious Land Use and Institutionalized Persons Act of 2000, 36 Weekly Comp. of Pres. Doc. 2168 (2000).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;RFRA and RLUIPA have restored part of the protection that <em>Smith</em> withdrew, but they are both limited in scope and can be weakened or repealed by Congress at any time.  They are no substitute for a proper interpretation of the Free Exercise Clause.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">III</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h3><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That project must begin with the constitutional text.  In <em>Martin </em>v.<em> Hunter's Lessee</em>, 1 Wheat. 304, 338-339 (1816), Justice Story laid down the guiding principle: "If the text be clear and distinct, no restriction upon its plain and obvious import ought to be admitted, unless the inference be irresistible."  And even though we now have a thick body of precedent regarding the meaning of most provisions of the Constitution, our opinions continue to respect the primacy of the Constitution's text.  See, <em>e.g</em>., <em>Chiafalo </em>v. <em>Washington</em>, 591 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___-___ (2020) (slip op., at 9-13) (starting with the text of Art. II, 1, before considering historical practice); <em>Knick</em> v. <em>Township of Scott</em>, 588 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2019) (slip op., at 6) (beginning analysis with the text of the Takings Clause); <em>Gamble </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 587 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___-___ (2019) (slip op., at 3-4) (starting with the text of the Fifth Amendment before turning to history and precedent); <em>City of Boerne</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S., at 519 ("In assessing the breadth of 5's enforcement power, we begin with its text").</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Smith, </em>however, paid shockingly little attention to the text of the Free Exercise Clause.  Instead of examining what readers would have understood its words to mean when adopted, the opinion merely asked whether it was "permissible" to read the text to have the meaning that the majority favored.  494 U.&nbsp;S., at 878.  This strange treatment of the constitutional text cannot be justified--and is especially surprising since it clashes so sharply with the way in which <em>Smith</em>'s author, Justice Scalia, generally treated the text of the Constitution (and, indeed, with his entire theory of legal interpretation).  As he put it, "What I look for in the Constitution is precisely what I look for in a statute:&nbsp;the original meaning of the text."  A. Scalia, A Matter of Interpretation 38 (1997).  See also <em>NLRB</em> v. <em>Noel Canning</em>, 573 U.&nbsp;S. 513, 575-583 (2014) (Scalia, J., concurring in judgment); <em>Stop the Beach Renourishment, Inc. </em>v. <em>Florida Dept. of Environmental Protection</em>, 560 U.&nbsp;S. 702, 722 (2010) (plurality opinion of Scalia, J.); <em>Maryland </em>v. <em>Craig</em>, 497 U.&nbsp;S. 836, 860-861 (1990) (Scalia, J., dissenting).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Justice Scalia's opinion for the Court in <em>District of Columbia </em>v.<em> Heller</em>, 554 U.&nbsp;S. 570 (2008), is a prime example of his usual approach, and it is a model of what a reexamination of the Free Exercise Clause should entail.  In <em>Heller</em>, after observing that the "Constitution was written to be understood by the voters," Justice Scalia's opinion begins by presuming that the "words and phrases" of the Second Amendment carry "their normal and ordinary .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. meaning."  <em>Id</em>., at 576 (internal quotation marks omitted).  The opinion then undertakes a careful examination of all the Amendment's key terms.  It does not simply ask whether its interpretation of the text is "permissible."  <em>Smith</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S., at 878.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Following the sound approach that the Court took in <em>Heller</em>, we should begin by considering the "normal and ordinary" meaning of the text of the Free Exercise Clause: "Congress shall make no law . . . prohibiting the free exercise [of religion]."  Most of these terms and phrases--"Congress,"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 27" name="FRopinion1.27" href="#FNopinion1.27">27</a></sup> "shall make," "no law,"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 28" name="FRopinion1.28" href="#FNopinion1.28">28</a></sup> and "religion"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 29" name="FRopinion1.29" href="#FNopinion1.29">29</a></sup>--do not require discussion for present purposes, and we can therefore focus on what remains: the term "prohibiting" and the phrase "the free exercise of religion."</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Those words had essentially the same meaning in 1791  as they do today.  "To prohibit" meant either "[t]o forbid" or "to hinder." 2 S. Johnson, A Dictionary of the English Language (1755) (Johnson (1755)).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 30" name="FRopinion1.30" href="#FNopinion1.30">30</a></sup>  The term "exercise" had both a broad primary definition ("[p]ractice" or "outward performance") and a narrower secondary one (an "[a]ct of divine worship whether publick or private").  1 <em>id</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 31" name="FRopinion1.31" href="#FNopinion1.31">31</a></sup>  (The Court long ago declined to give the First Amendment's reference to "exercise" this narrow reading.  See, <em>e.g</em>., <em>Cantwell </em>v.<em> Connecticut, </em>310 U.&nbsp;S. 296, 303-304 (1940).)  And "free," in the sense relevant here, meant "unrestrained."  1 Johnson (1755).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 32" name="FRopinion1.32" href="#FNopinion1.32">32</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;If we put these definitions together, the ordinary meaning of "prohibiting the free exercise of religion" was (and still is) forbidding or hindering unrestrained religious practices or worship.  That straightforward understanding is a far cry from the interpretation adopted in <em>Smith</em>.  It certainly does not suggest a distinction between laws that are generally applicable and laws that are targeted.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As interpreted in <em>Smith</em>, the Clause is essentially an anti-discrimination provision: It means that the Federal Government and the States cannot restrict conduct that constitutes a religious practice for some people unless it imposes the same restriction on everyone else who engages in the same conduct.  <em>Smith</em> made no real attempt to square that equal-treatment interpretation with the ordinary meaning of the Free Exercise Clause's language, and it is hard to see how that could be done.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The key point for present purposes is that the text of the Free Exercise Clause gives a specific group of people (those who wish to engage in the "exercise of religion") the right to do so without hindrance.  The language of the Clause does not tie this right to the treatment of persons not in this group.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The oddity of <em>Smith</em>'s interpretation can be illustrated by considering what the same sort of interpretation would mean if applied to other provisions of the Bill of Rights.  Take the Sixth Amendment, which gives a specified group of people (the "accused" in criminal cases) a particular right (the right to the "Assistance of Counsel for [their] defence").  Suppose that Congress or a state legislature adopted a law banning counsel in <em>all litigation</em>, civil and criminal.  Would anyone doubt that this law would violate the Sixth Amendment rights of criminal defendants?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Or consider the Seventh Amendment, which gives a specified group of people (parties in most civil "Suits at common law") "the right of trial by jury."  Would there be any question that a law abolishing juries in <em>all</em> civil cases would violate the rights of parties in cases that fall within the Seventh Amendment's scope?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Other examples involving language similar to that in the Free Exercise Clause are easy to imagine.  Suppose that the amount of time generally allotted to complete a state bar exam is 12 hours but that applicants with disabilities secure a consent decree allowing them an extra hour.  Suppose that the State later adopts a rule requiring all applicants to complete the exam in 11 hours.  Would anyone argue that this was consistent with the decree?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Suppose that classic car enthusiasts secure the passage of a state constitutional amendment exempting cars of a certain age from annual safety inspections, but the legislature later enacts a law requiring such inspections for all vehicles regardless of age.  Can there be any doubt that this would violate the state constitution?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is not necessary to belabor this point further.  What all these examples show is that <em>Smith</em>'s interpretation conflicts with the ordinary meaning of the First Amendment's terms.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Is there any way to bring about a reconciliation?  The short answer is "no."  Survey all the briefs filed in support of respondents (they total more than 40) and three decades of law review articles, and what will you find?  Philadelphia's brief refers in passing to one possible argument--and the source it cites is a law review article by one of <em>Smith</em>'s leading academic critics, Professor Michael W. McConnell.  See Brief for City Respondents 49 (citing McConnell, Free Exercise Revisionism 1115).  Trying to see if there was any way to make <em>Smith</em> fit with the constitutional text, Professor McConnell came up with this argument--but then rejected it.  McConnell<em>, </em>Free Exercise Revisionism 1115-1116.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The argument goes as follows: Even if a law prohibits conduct that constitutes an essential religious practice, it cannot be said to "prohibit" the free exercise of religion unless that was the lawmakers' specific object.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This is a hair-splitting interpretation.  It certainly does not represent the "normal and ordinary" meaning of the Free Exercise Clause's terms.  See <em>Heller</em>, 554 U.&nbsp;S., at 576.  Consider how it would play out if applied to some of the hypothetical laws discussed at the beginning of this opinion.  A law categorically banning all wine would not "prohibit" the celebration of a Catholic Mass?  A law categorically forbidding the slaughter of a conscious animal would not "prohibit" kosher and halal slaughterhouses?  A rule categorically banning any head covering in a courtroom would not "prohibit" appearances by orthodox Jewish men, Sikh men, and Muslim women who wear hijabs?  It is no wonder that <em>Smith</em>'s many defenders have almost uniformly foregone this argument.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">D</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Not only is it difficult to square <em>Smith</em>'s interpretation with the terms of the Free Exercise Clause, the absence of any language referring to equal treatment is striking.  If equal treatment was the objective, why didn't Congress say that?  And since it would have been simple to cast the Free Exercise Clause in equal-treatment terms, why would the state legislators who voted for ratification have read the Clause that way?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is not as if there were no models that could have been used.  Other constitutional provisions contain non-discrimination language.  For example, Art. I, 9, cl.&nbsp;6, provides that "[n]o Preference shall be given by any Regulation of Commerce or Revenue to the Ports of one State over those of another."  Under Art. IV, 2, cl.&nbsp;1, "[t]he Citizens of each State shall be entitled to all Privileges and Immunities of Citizens in the several States."  Article V provides that "no State, without its Consent, shall be deprived of its equal Suffrage in the Senate."  Language mandating equal treatment of one sort or another also appeared in the religious liberty provisions of colonial charters and state constitutions.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 33" name="FRopinion1.33" href="#FNopinion1.33">33</a></sup>  But Congress eschewed those models.  The contrast between these readily available anti-discrimination models and the language that appears in the First Amendment speaks volumes.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">IV</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h3><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;While we presume that the words of the Constitution carry their ordinary and normal meaning, we cannot disregard the possibility that some of the terms in the Free Exercise Clause had a special meaning that was well understood at the time.  <em>Heller</em>, again, provides a helpful example.  <em>Heller</em> did not hold that the right to keep and bear arms means that everyone has the right to keep and bear every type of weaponry in all places and at all times.  Instead, it held that the Second Amendment protects a known right that was understood to have defined dimensions.  554 U.&nbsp;S., at 626-628.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Following <em>Heller</em>'s lead, we must ask whether the Free Exercise Clause protects a right that was known at the time of adoption to have defined dimensions.  But in doing so, we must keep in mind that there is a presumption that the words of the Constitution are to be interpreted in accordance with their "normal and ordinary" sense.  <em>Id.</em>, at 576 (internal quotation marks omitted).  Anyone advocating a different reading must overcome that presumption.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h3><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;What was the free-exercise right understood to mean when the Bill of Rights was ratified?  And in particular, was it clearly understood that the right simply required equal treatment for religious and secular conduct?  When <em>Smith</em> was decided, scholars had not devoted much attention to the original meaning of the Free Exercise Clause, and the parties' briefs ignored this issue, as did the opinion of the Court.  Since then, however, the historical record has been plumbed in detail,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 34" name="FRopinion1.34" href="#FNopinion1.34">34</a></sup> and we are now in a good position to examine how the free-exercise right was understood when the First Amendment was adopted.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;By that date, the right to religious liberty already had a long, rich, and complex history in this country.  What appears to be the first "free exercise" provision was adopted in 1649.  Prompted by Lord Baltimore,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 35" name="FRopinion1.35" href="#FNopinion1.35">35</a></sup> the Maryland Assembly enacted a provision protecting the right of all Christians to engage in "the free exercise" of religion.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 36" name="FRopinion1.36" href="#FNopinion1.36">36</a></sup>  Rhode Island's 1663 Charter extended the right to all.  See Charter of Rhode Island and Providence Plantations (1663), in Cogan 34.  Early colonial charters and agreements in Carolina, Delaware, New Jersey, New York, and Pennsylvania also recognized the right to free exercise,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 37" name="FRopinion1.37" href="#FNopinion1.37">37</a></sup> and by 1789, every State except Connecticut had a constitutional provision protecting religious liberty.  McConnell, Origins 1455.  In fact, the Free Exercise Clause had more analogs in State Constitutions than any other individual right.  See Calabresi, Agudo, &amp; Dore, State Bills of Rights in 1787 and 1791: What Individual Rights Are Really Deeply Rooted in American History and Tradition? 85 S. Cal. L. Rev. 1451, 1463-1464, 1472-1473 (2012).  In all of those State Constitutions, freedom of religion enjoyed broad protection, and the right "was universally said to be an unalienable right."  McConnell, Origins 1456.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 38" name="FRopinion1.38" href="#FNopinion1.38">38</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;What was this right understood to protect?  In seeking to discern that meaning, it is easy to get lost in the voluminous discussion of religious liberty that occurred during the long period from the first British settlements to the adoption of the Bill of Rights.  Many different political figures, religious leaders, and others spoke and wrote about religious liberty and the relationship between the authority of civil governments and religious bodies.  The works of a variety of thinkers were influential, and views on religious liberty were informed by religion, philosophy, historical experience, particular controversies and issues, and in no small measure by the practical task of uniting the Nation.  The picture is complex.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For present purposes, we can narrow our focus and concentrate on the circumstances that relate most directly to the adoption of the Free Exercise Clause.  As has often been recounted, critical state ratifying conventions approved the Constitution on the understanding that it would be amended to provide express protection for certain fundamental rights,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 39" name="FRopinion1.39" href="#FNopinion1.39">39</a></sup> and the right to religious liberty was unquestionably one of those rights.  As noted, it was expressly protected in 12 of the 13 State Constitutions, and these state constitutional provisions provide the best evidence of the scope of the right embodied in the First Amendment.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;When we look at these provisions, we see one predominant model.  This model extends broad protection for religious liberty but expressly provides that the right does not protect conduct that would endanger "the public peace" or "safety."</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This model had deep roots in early colonial charters.  It appeared in the Rhode Island Charter of 1663,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 40" name="FRopinion1.40" href="#FNopinion1.40">40</a></sup> the Second Charter of Carolina in 1665,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 41" name="FRopinion1.41" href="#FNopinion1.41">41</a></sup> and the New York Act Declaring Rights &amp; Priviledges in 1691.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 42" name="FRopinion1.42" href="#FNopinion1.42">42</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;By the founding, more than half of the State Constitutions contained free-exercise provisions subject to a "peace and safety" carveout or something similar.  The Georgia Constitution is a good example.  It provided that "[a]ll persons whatever shall have the free exercise of their religion; provided it be not repugnant to the <em>peace and safety</em> of the State."  Ga. Const., Art. LVI (1777), in Cogan 16 (emphasis added).  The founding era Constitutions of Delaware, Maryland, Massachusetts, New Hampshire, New York, Rhode Island, and South Carolina all contained broad protections for religious exercise, subject to limited peace-and-safety carveouts.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 43" name="FRopinion1.43" href="#FNopinion1.43">43</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The predominance of this model is highlighted by its use in the laws governing the Northwest Territory.  In the Northwest Ordinance of 1787, the Continental Congress provided that "[n]o person, demeaning himself in <em>a peaceable and orderly manner</em>, shall ever be molested on account of his mode of worship, or religious sentiments, in the said territory."  Art. I (emphasis added).  After the ratification of the Constitution, the First Congress used similar language in the Northwest Ordinance of 1789.  See Act of Aug. 7, 1789, 1 Stat. 52 (reaffirming Art. I of Northwest Ordinance of 1787).  Since the First Congress also framed and approved the Bill of Rights, we have often said that its apparent understanding of the scope of those rights is entitled to great respect.  See, <em>e.g., Town of Greece</em> v. <em>Galloway</em>, 572 U.&nbsp;S. 565, 575-578 (2014); <em>Harmelin</em> v. <em>Michigan</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 957, 980 (1991) (opinion of Scalia, J.); <em>Marsh</em> v. <em>Chambers</em>, 463 U.&nbsp;S. 783, 786-792 (1983); <em>Carroll</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 267 U.&nbsp;S. 132, 150-151 (1925).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">3</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The model favored by Congress and the state legislatures--providing broad protection for the free exercise of religion except where public "peace" or "safety" would be endangered--is antithetical to <em>Smith.  </em>If, as <em>Smith</em> held, the free-exercise right does not require any religious exemptions from generally applicable laws, it is not easy to imagine situations in which a public-peace-or-safety carveout would be necessary.  Legislatures enact generally applicable laws to protect public peace and safety.  If those laws are thought to be sufficient to address a particular type of conduct when engaged in for a secular purpose, why wouldn't they also be sufficient to address the same type of conduct when carried out for a religious reason?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Smith</em>'s defenders have no good answer.  Their chief response is that the free-exercise provisions that included these carveouts were tantamount to the <em>Smith</em> rule because any conduct that is generally prohibited or generally required can be regarded as necessary to protect public peace or safety.  See <em>City of Boerne</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S., at 539 (Scalia, J., concurring in part) ("At the time these provisos were enacted, keeping 'peace' and 'order' seems to have meant, precisely, obeying the laws").</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This argument gives "public peace and safety" an unnaturally broad interpretation.  Samuel Johnson's 1755 dictionary defined "peace" as: "1. Respite from war.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;. 2. Quiet from suits or disturbances.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;. 3. Rest from any commotion. 4. Stil[l]ness from riots or tumults.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;. 5. Reconciliation of differences.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;. 6. A state not hostile.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;. 7. Rest; quiet; content; freedom from terrour; heavenly rest.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;."  2 Johnson.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 44" name="FRopinion1.44" href="#FNopinion1.44">44</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In ordinary usage, the term "safety" was understood to mean: "1. Freedom from danger. .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. 2. Exemption from hurt. 3. Preservation from hurt. .&nbsp;.&nbsp;."  <em>Ibid</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 45" name="FRopinion1.45" href="#FNopinion1.45">45</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;When "peace" and "safety" are understood in this way, it cannot be said that every violation of every law imperils public "peace" or "safety."  In 1791 (and today), violations of many laws do not threaten "war," "disturbances," "commotion," "riots," "terrour," "danger," or "hurt."  Blackstone catalogs numerous violations that do not threaten any such harms, including  "cursing";<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 46" name="FRopinion1.46" href="#FNopinion1.46">46</a></sup>  refusing to pay assessments for "the repairs of sea banks and sea walls" and the "cleansing of rivers, public streams, ditches and other conduits";<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 47" name="FRopinion1.47" href="#FNopinion1.47">47</a></sup> "retaining a man's hired servant before his time is expired";<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 48" name="FRopinion1.48" href="#FNopinion1.48">48</a></sup> an attorney's failure to show up for a trial;<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 49" name="FRopinion1.49" href="#FNopinion1.49">49</a></sup> the unauthorized "solemniz[ing of a] marriage in any other place besides a church, or public chapel wherein banns have been usually published";<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 50" name="FRopinion1.50" href="#FNopinion1.50">50</a></sup> "transporting and seducing our artists to settle abroad";<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 51" name="FRopinion1.51" href="#FNopinion1.51">51</a></sup> engaging in the conduct of "a common scold";<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 52" name="FRopinion1.52" href="#FNopinion1.52">52</a></sup> and "exercis[ing] a trade in any town, without having previously served as an apprentice for seven years."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 53" name="FRopinion1.53" href="#FNopinion1.53">53</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In contrast to these violations, Blackstone lists "offences against the public peace." 4 Commentaries on the Laws of England 142-153 (1769).  Those include: riotous assembling of 12 persons or more; unlawful hunting; anonymous threats and demands; destruction of public floodgates, locks, or sluices on a navigable river; public fighting; riots or unlawful assemblies; "tumultuous" petitioning; forcible entry or detainer; riding or "going armed" with dangerous or unusual weapons; spreading false news to "make discord between the king and nobility, or concerning any great man of the realm"; spreading "false and pretended" prophecies to disturb the peace; provoking breaches of the peace; and libel "to provoke . . . wrath, or expose [an individual] to public hatred, contempt, and ridicule."  <em>Ibid. </em>(emphasis deleted); see also McConnell, Freedom from Persecution 835-836.  These offenses might inform what constitutes actual or threatened breaches of public peace or safety in the ordinary sense of those terms.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 54" name="FRopinion1.54" href="#FNopinion1.54">54</a></sup>  But the ordinary meaning of offenses that threaten public peace or safety must be stretched beyond the breaking point to encompass <em>all </em>violations of <em>any</em> law.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 55" name="FRopinion1.55" href="#FNopinion1.55">55</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That the free-exercise right included the right to certain religious exemptions is strongly supported by the practice of the Colonies and States.  When there were important clashes between generally applicable laws and the religious practices of particular groups, colonial and state legislatures were willing to grant exemptions--even when the generally applicable laws served critical state interests.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Oath exemptions are illustrative.  Oath requirements were considered "indispensable" to civil society because they were thought to ensure that individuals gave truthful testimony and fulfilled commitments.  McConnell, Origins 1467.  Quakers and members of some other religious groups refused to take oaths, <em>ibid.</em>, and therefore a categorical oath requirement would have resulted in the complete exclusion of these Americans from important civic activities, such as testifying in court and voting, see <em>ibid.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Tellingly, that is not what happened.  In the 1600s, Carolina allowed Quakers to enter a pledge rather than swearing an oath.  <em>Ibid.</em>  In 1691, New York permitted Quakers to give testimony after giving an affirmation.  <em>Ibid.</em>  Massachusetts did the same in 1743.  <em>Id.,</em> at 1467-1468.  In 1734, New York also allowed Quakers to qualify to vote by making an affirmation, and in 1740, Georgia granted an exemption to Jews, allowing them to omit the phrase "&nbsp;'on the faith of a Christian'&nbsp;" from the State's naturalization oath.  <em>Id.,</em> at 1467.  By 1789, almost all States had passed oath exemptions.  <em>Id.</em>, at 1468.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Some early State Constitutions and declarations of rights formally provided oath exemptions for religious objectors.  For instance, the Maryland Declaration of Rights of 1776 declared that Quakers, Mennonites, and members of some other religious groups "ought to be allowed to make their solemn affirmation" instead of an oath.  36, in Cogan 18.  Similarly, the Massachusetts Constitution of 1780 permitted Quakers holding certain government positions to decline to take the prescribed oath of office, allowing affirmations instead.  Pt. II, ch. VI, Art. I, in <em>id.,</em> at 22.  The Federal Constitution likewise permits federal and state officials to make either an "Oath <em>or </em>Affirmation, to support this Constitution."  Art.&nbsp;VI, cl. 3 (emphasis added); see also Art. I, 3, cl. 6; Art. II, 1, cl. 8.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Military conscription provides an even more revealing example.  In the Colonies and later in the States, able-bodied men of a certain age were required to serve in the militia, see <em>Heller</em>, 554 U.&nbsp;S., at 595-596, but Quakers, Mennonites, and members of some other religious groups objected to militia service on religious grounds, see McConnell, Origins 1468.  The militia was regarded as essential to the security of the State and the preservation of freedom, see <em>Heller</em>, 554 U.&nbsp;S., at 597-598, but colonial governments nevertheless granted religious exemptions, see McConnell, Origins 1468.  Rhode Island, Maryland, North Carolina, and New Hampshire did so in the founding era.  <em>Ibid.  </em>In 1755, New York permitted a conscientious objector to obtain an exemption if he paid a fee or sent a substitute.  <em>Ibid.</em>  Massachusetts adopted a similar law two years later, and Virginia followed suit in 1776.  <em>Ibid.</em>, and n. 297.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Continental Congress also granted exemptions to religious objectors because conscription would do "violence to their consciences."  Resolution of July 18, 1775, in<em> </em>2 Journals of the Continental Congress, 1774-1789, p. 189 (W. Ford ed. 1905) (quoted in McConnell, Origins 1469, and n. 299).  This decision is especially revealing because during that time the Continental Army was periodically in desperate need of soldiers,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 56" name="FRopinion1.56" href="#FNopinion1.56">56</a></sup> the very survival of the new Nation  </p>
<p>often seemed in danger,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 57" name="FRopinion1.57" href="#FNopinion1.57">57</a></sup> and the Members of Congress faced bleak personal prospects if the war was lost.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 58" name="FRopinion1.58" href="#FNopinion1.58">58</a></sup>  Yet despite these stakes, exemptions were granted.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Colonies with established churches also permitted non-members to decline to pay special taxes dedicated to the support of ministers of the established church.  McConnell, Origins 1469.  Massachusetts and Connecticut exempted Baptists and Quakers in 1727.  <em>Ibid.</em>  Virginia provided exemptions to Huguenots in 1700, German Lutherans in 1730, and dissenters from the Church of England in 1776.  <em>Ibid.</em>; see also S. Cobb, The Rise of Religious Liberty in America 98, 492 (1902).  Beginning in 1692, New Hampshire exempted those who could prove they were "&nbsp;'conscientiously'&nbsp;" of a "&nbsp;'different persuasion,'&nbsp;" regularly attended their own religious services, and contributed financially to their faith.  McConnell<em>, </em>Origins 1469<em>.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Various other religious exemptions were also provided.  North Carolina and Maryland granted exemptions from the requirement that individuals remove their hats in court, a gesture that Quakers viewed as an impermissible showing of respect to a secular authority.  <em>Id.</em>, at 1471-1472.  And Rhode Island exempted Jews from some marriage laws.  <em>Id.</em>, at 1471.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In an effort to dismiss the significance of these legislative exemptions, it has been argued that they show only what the Constitution permits, not what it requires.  <em>City of Boerne</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S., at 541 (opinion of Scalia, J.).  But legislatures provided those accommodations before the concept of judicial review took hold, and their actions are therefore strong evidence of the founding era's understanding of the free-exercise right.  See McConnell<em>, </em>Free Exercise Revisionism 1119.  Cf. <em>Heller</em>, 554 U.&nbsp;S., at 600-603 (looking to state constitutions that preceded the adoption of the Second Amendment).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">D</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Defenders of <em>Smith </em>have advanced<em> </em>historical arguments of their own, but they are unconvincing, and in any event, plainly insufficient to overcome the ordinary meaning of the constitutional text.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;One prominent argument points to language in some founding-era charters and constitutions prohibiting laws or government actions that were taken "for" or "on account" of religion.  See <em>City of Boerne</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S., at 538-539 (opinion of Scalia, J.).  That phrasing, it is argued, reaches only measures that target religion, not neutral and generally applicable laws.  This argument has many flaws.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;No such language appears in the Free Exercise Clause, and in any event, the argument rests on a crabbed reading of the words "for" or "on account of&nbsp;" religion.  As Professor McConnell has explained, "[i]f a member of the Native American Church is arrested for ingesting peyote during a religious ceremony, then he surely is molested 'for' or 'on account of&nbsp;' his religious practice--even though the law under which he is arrested is neutral and generally applicable."  Freedom From Persecution 834.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This argument also ignores the full text of many of the provisions on which it relies.  <em>Id.,</em> at 833-834.  While some protect against government actions taken "for" or "on account of&nbsp;" religion, they do not stop there.  Instead, they go on to provide broader protection for religious liberty.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> Maryland Act Concerning Religion (1649), in Cogan 17 (guaranteeing residents not be "troubled .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. in the free exercise [of religion]"); New York Constitution (1777), in <em>id.</em>, at 26 (guaranteeing "the free Exercise and Enjoyment of religious Profession and Worship").</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Another argument advanced by <em>Smith</em>'s defenders relies on the paucity of early cases "refusing to enforce a generally applicable statute because of its failure to make accommodation," <em>City of Boerne</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S., at 542 (opinion of Scalia, J.).  If exemptions were thought to be constitutionally required, they contend, we would see many such cases.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;There might be something to this argument if there were a great many cases denying exemptions and few granting them, but the fact is that diligent research has found only a handful of cases going either way.  Commentators have discussed the dearth of cases, and as they note, there are many possible explanations.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 59" name="FRopinion1.59" href="#FNopinion1.59">59</a></sup>  Early 19th century legislation imposed only limited restrictions on private conduct, and this minimized the chances of conflict between generally applicable laws and religious practices.  The principal conflicts that arose--involving oaths, conscription, and taxes to support an established church--were largely resolved by state constitutional provisions and laws granting exemptions.  And the religious demographics of the time decreased the likelihood of conflicts.  The population was overwhelmingly Christian and Protestant, the major Protestant denominations made up the great bulk of the religious adherents,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 60" name="FRopinion1.60" href="#FNopinion1.60">60</a></sup> and other than with respect to the issue of taxes to support an established church, it is hard to think of conflicts between the practices of the members of these denominations and generally applicable laws that a state legislature might have enacted.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Members of minority religions are most likely to encounter such conflicts, and the largest minority group, the Quakers, who totaled about 10% of religious adherents,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 61" name="FRopinion1.61" href="#FNopinion1.61">61</a></sup> had received exemptions for the practices that conflicted with generally applicable laws.  As will later be shown, see <em>infra</em>, at 46-50, the small number of religious-exemption cases that occurred during the early 19th century involved members of what were then tiny religious groups--such as Catholics, Jews, and Covenanters.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 62" name="FRopinion1.62" href="#FNopinion1.62">62</a></sup>  Given the size of these groups, one would not expect a large number of cases.  And where cases arose, the courts' decisions may not have always been reported.  Barclay, The Historical Origins of Judicial Religious Exemptions, 96 Notre Dame L. Rev. 55, 70 (2020).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">3</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;When the body of potentially relevant cases is examined, they provide little support for <em>Smith</em>'s interpretation of the free-exercise right.  Not only are these decisions few in number, but they reached mixed results.  In addition, some are unreasoned; some provide ambiguous explanations; and many of the cases denying exemptions were based on grounds that do not support <em>Smith.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The most influential early case granting an exemption was <em>People</em> v. <em>Philips</em>, 1 W. L.&nbsp;J. 109, 112-113 (Gen. Sess., N.&nbsp;Y. 1813), where the court held that a Catholic priest could not be compelled to testify about a confession.  The priest's refusal, the court reasoned, was protected by the state constitutional right to the free exercise of religion and did not fall within the exception for "acts of licentiousness" and "practices inconsistent with the peace or safety of th[e] State."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 63" name="FRopinion1.63" href="#FNopinion1.63">63</a></sup>  This, of course, is exactly the understanding of the free-exercise right that is seen in the founding era State Constitutions. </p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Although <em>Philips</em> was not officially reported, knowledge of the decision appears to have spread widely.  Four years later, another New York court implicitly reaffirmed the principle <em>Philips</em> recognized but found the decision inapplicable because the Protestant minister who was called to testify did not feel a religious obligation to refuse.  See <em>Smith</em>'<em>s Case</em>, 2 N.&nbsp;Y. City-Hall Recorder 77, 80, and n. (1817); McConnell, Origins 1505-1506; Walsh 40-41.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In 1827, a South Carolina court relied on <em>Philips </em>as support for its decision to grant an exemption from a state law relied on to bar the testimony of a witness who denied a belief in punishment after death for testifying falsely, and the State's newly constituted high court approved that opinion.  <em>Farnandis </em>v.<em> Henderson</em>, 1 Carolina L. J. 202, 213, 214 (1827).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 64" name="FRopinion1.64" href="#FNopinion1.64">64</a></sup> </p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In <em>Commonwealth </em>v.<em> Cronin</em>, 2 Va. Cir. 488, 498, 500, 505 (1855), a Virginia court followed <em>Philips</em> and held that a priest's free-exercise right required an exemption from the general common law rule compelling a witness to "disclose all he may know" when giving testimony.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;On the other side of the ledger, the most prominent opponent of exemptions was John Bannister Gibson of the Pennsylvania Supreme Court.  Today, Gibson is best known for his dissent in <em>Eakin </em>v<em>. Raub</em>, 12 Serg. &amp; Rawle 330, 355-356 (1825),&nbsp;which challenged John Marshall's argument for judicial review in <em>Marbury </em>v<em>. Madison</em>, 1 Cranch 137 (1803).  See McConnell, Origins 1507.  Three years after <em>Eakin</em>, Gibson's dissent in <em>Commonwealth</em> v. <em>Lesher</em>, 17 Serg. &amp; Rawle 155 (Pa. 1828), advanced a related argument against decisions granting religious exemptions.  Gibson agreed that the state constitutional provision protecting religious liberty conferred the right to do or forbear from doing any act "not prejudicial to the public weal," but he argued that judges had no authority to override legislative judgments about what the public weal required. <em> Id.,</em> at 160-161 (emphasis deleted).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Three years later, he made a similar argument in dicta in <em>Philips's Executors </em>v. <em>Gratz</em>, 2 Pen. &amp; W. 412, 412-413 (Pa. 1831), where a Jewish plaintiff had taken a non-suit (agreed to a dismissal) in a civil case scheduled for trial on a Saturday.  Gibson's opinion for the Court set aside the non-suit on other grounds but rejected the plaintiff&nbsp;'s religious objection to trial on Saturday.  <em>Id.,</em> at 416-417.  He proclaimed that a citizen's obligation to the State must always take precedence over any religious obligation, and he expressly registered disagreement with the New York court's decision in <em>Philips</em>.  <em>Id.,</em> at 417.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In South Carolina, an exemption claim was denied in <em>State </em>v.<em> Willson</em>, 13 S. C. L. 393, 394-397 (1823), where the court refused to exempt a member of the Covenanters religious movement from jury service.  Because Covenanters opposed the Constitution on religious grounds, they refused to engage in activities, such as jury service and voting, that required an oath to support the Constitution or otherwise enlisted their participation in the Nation's scheme of government.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 65" name="FRopinion1.65" href="#FNopinion1.65">65</a></sup>  It is possible to read the opinion in <em>Willson</em>  as embodying something like the <em>Smith</em> rule--or as concluding that granting the exemption would have opened the floodgates and undermined public peace and safety.  See 13 S. C. L., at 395 ("who could distinguish .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. between the pious asseveration of a holy man and that of an accomplished villain").  But if <em>Willson</em> is read as rejecting religious exemptions, South Carolina's reconstituted high court reversed that position in <em>Farnandis</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 66" name="FRopinion1.66" href="#FNopinion1.66">66</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Other cases denying exemptions are even less helpful to <em>Smith</em>'s defenders.  Three decisions rejected challenges to Sunday closing laws by merchants who celebrated Saturday as the Sabbath, but at least two of these were based on the court's conclusion that the asserted religious belief was unfounded.  See <em>City Council of Charleston</em> v. <em>Benjamin</em>, 33 S. C. L. 508, 529 (1846) ("There is .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. no violation of the Hebrew's religion, in requiring him to cease from labor on another day than his Sabbath, if he be left free to observe the latter according to his religion" (emphasis deleted)); <em>Commonwealth</em> v. <em>Wolf</em>, 3 Serg. &amp; Rawle 47, 50, 51 (Pa. 1817) ("[T]he Jewish Talmud .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. asserts no such doctrine" and the objection was made "out of mere caprice").  That reasoning is contrary to a principle that <em>Smith</em> reaffirmed: "Repeatedly and in many different contexts, we have warned that courts must not presume to determine .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. the plausibility of a religious claim."  494 U.&nbsp;S., at 887.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;A third Sunday closing law decision appears to rest at least in part on a similar ground.  See <em>Specht </em>v.<em> Commonwealth</em>, 8 Pa. 312 (1848).  The court observed that the merchant's conscience rights might have been violated if his religion actually required him to work on Sunday, but the court concluded that the commandment to keep holy the Sabbath had never been understood to impose "an imperative obligation to fill up each day of the other six with some worldly employment."  <em>Id</em>., at 326.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Other cases cited as denying exemptions were decided on nebulous grounds.  In <em>Stansbury</em> v<em>. Marks</em>, 2 Dall. 213 (Pa. 1793), a decision of the Pennsylvania Supreme Court, the case report in its entirety states: "In this cause (which was tried on Saturday, the 5th of April) the defendant offered Jonas Phillips, a Jew, as a witness; but he refused to be sworn, because it was his Sabbath.  The Court, therefore, fined him 10; but the defendant, afterwards, waving the benefit of his testimony, he was discharged from the fine."  (Emphasis deleted.)  What can be deduced from this cryptic summary?  Was the issue mooted when the defendant waived the benefit of Phillips's testimony?  Who can tell?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In <em>Commonwealth</em> v. <em>Drake</em>, 15 Mass. 161 (1818), the Supreme Judicial Court of Massachusetts summarily affirmed the conviction of a criminal defendant who was convicted after the trial court admitted the testimony of his fellow church members before whom he had confessed.  The State argued that the defendant had voluntarily confessed, that his confession was not required by any "ecclesiastical rule," and that he had confessed "not to the church" but "to his friends and neighbours."  <em>Id.</em>, at 162.  Because the court provided no explanation of its decision, this case sheds no light on the understanding of the free-exercise right.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;All told, this mixed bag of antebellum decisions does little to support <em>Smith</em>, and extending the search past the Civil War does not advance <em>Smith</em>'s cause.  One of the objectives of the Fourteenth Amendment, it has been argued, was to protect the religious liberty of African-Americans in the South, where a combination of laws that did not facially target religious practice had been used to suppress religious exercise by slaves.  See generally Lash, The Second Adoption of the Free Exercise Clause: Religious Exemptions Under the Fourteenth Amendment, 88 Nw. U. L. Rev. 1106 (1994).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">4</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Some have claimed that the drafting history of the Bill of Rights supports <em>Smith</em>.  See Brief for First Amendment Scholars as <em>Amici Curiae </em>10-11; Muoz, Original Meaning 1085.  But as Professor Philip Hamburger, one of <em>Smith</em>'s most prominent academic defenders, has concluded, "[w]hat any of this [history] implies about the meaning of the Free Exercise Clause is speculative."  Religious Exemption 928.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Here is the relevant history.  The House debated a provision, originally proposed by Madison, that protected the right to bear arms but included language stating that "no person, religiously scrupulous, shall be compelled to bear arms."  1 Annals of Cong. 749, 766 (1789); see also Muoz, Original Meaning 1112.  Some Members spoke in favor of </p>
<p>the proposal,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 67" name="FRopinion1.67" href="#FNopinion1.67">67</a></sup> others opposed it,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 68" name="FRopinion1.68" href="#FNopinion1.68">68</a></sup> and in the end, after adding the words "in person" at the end of the clause, the House adopted it.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 69" name="FRopinion1.69" href="#FNopinion1.69">69</a></sup>  The Senate, however, rejected the proposal (for reasons not provided on the public record), <em>id</em>., at 1116, and the House acceded to the deletion.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Those who claim that this episode supports <em>Smith</em> argue that the House would not have found it necessary to include this proviso in the Second Amendment if it had thought that the Free Exercise Clause already protected conscientious objectors from conscription, Muoz, Original Meaning 1120, but that conclusion is unfounded.  Those who favored Madison's language might have thought it necessary, not because the free-exercise right <em>never</em> required religious exemptions but because they feared that exemption from military service would be held to fall into the free-exercise right's carveout for conduct that threatens public safety.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 70" name="FRopinion1.70" href="#FNopinion1.70">70</a></sup>  And of course, it could be argued that the willingness of the House to constitutionalize this exemption despite its potential effect on national security shows the depth of the Members' commitment to the concept of religious exemptions.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As for the Senate's rejection of the proviso, we have often warned against drawing inferences from Congress's failure to adopt a legislative proposal.  See <em>Schneidewind </em>v. <em>ANR Pipeline Co.</em>, 485 U.&nbsp;S. 293, 306 (1988) ("This Court generally is reluctant to draw inferences from Congress' failure to act"); <em>Brecht </em>v. <em>Abrahamson</em>, 507 U.&nbsp;S. 619, 632-633 (1993) (collecting cases).  And in this instance, there are many possible explanations for what happened in the Senate.  The rejection of the proviso <em>could</em> have been due to a general objection to religious exemptions, but it could also have been based on any of the following grounds: opposition to this particular exemption, the belief that conscientious objectors were already protected by the Free Exercise Clause, a belief that military service fell within the public safety carveout, or the view that Congress should be able to decide whether to grant or withhold such exemptions based on its assessment of what national security required at particular times.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">***</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In sum, based on the text of the Free Exercise Clause and evidence about the original understanding of the free-exercise right, the case for <em>Smith </em>fails to overcome the more natural reading of the text.  Indeed, the case against <em>Smith</em> is very convincing.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">V</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That conclusion cannot end our analysis.  "We will not overturn a past decision unless there are strong grounds for doing so," <em>Janus </em>v.<em> State, County, and Municipal Employees, </em>585 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2018) (slip op., at 34), but at the same time, <em>stare decisis </em>is "not an inexorable command."  <em>Ibid.</em> (internal quotation marks omitted).  It "is at its weakest when we interpret the Constitution because our interpretation can be altered only by constitutional amendment or by overruling our prior decisions."  <em>Agostini </em>v.<em> Felton</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 203, 235 (1997).  And it applies with "perhaps least force of all to decisions that wrongly denied First Amendment rights."  <em>Janus</em>, 585 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 34); see also<em> Federal Election Comm'n </em>v. <em>Wisconsin Right to Life, Inc.</em>, 551 U.&nbsp;S. 449, 500 (2007) (Scalia, J., concurring in part and concurring in judgment) ("This Court has not hesitated to overrule decisions offensive to the First Amendment (a fixed star in our constitutional constellation, if there is one)" (internal quotation marks omitted)); <em>Citizens United </em>v. <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em>, 558 U.&nbsp;S. 310, 365 (2010) (overruling <em>Austin</em> v. <em>Michigan Chamber of Commerce</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S. 652 (1990)); <em>West Virginia Bd. of Ed. </em>v.<em> Barnette</em>, 319 U. S. 624, 642 (1943) (overruling <em>Minersville School Dist. </em>v<em>. Gobitis</em>, 310 U. S. 586 (1940)).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In assessing whether to overrule a past decision that appears to be incorrect, we have considered a variety of factors, and four of those weigh strongly against <em>Smith</em>: its reasoning; its consistency with other decisions; the workability of the rule that it established; and developments since the decision was handed down.  See <em>Janus</em>, 585 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___ (slip op., at 34-35).  No relevant factor, including reliance, weighs in <em>Smith</em>'s favor.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Smith's reasoning</em>.  As explained in detail above, <em>Smith</em> is a methodological outlier.  It ignored the "normal and ordinary" meaning of the constitutional text, see <em>Heller</em>, 554 U.&nbsp;S., at 576, and it made no real effort to explore the understanding of the free-exercise right at the time of the First Amendment's adoption.  And the Court adopted its reading of the Free Exercise Clause with no briefing on the issue from the parties or <em>amici</em>.  Laycock, 8 J. L. &amp; Religion, at 101.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Then there is <em>Smith</em>'s treatment of precedent.  It looked for precedential support in strange places, and the many precedents that stood in its way received remarkably rough treatment.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Looking for a case that had endorsed its no-exemptions view,<em> Smith</em> turned to <em>Gobitis</em>, 310 U.&nbsp;S., at 586, a decision that Justice Scalia himself later acknowledged was "erroneous," <em>Wisconsin Right to Life</em>, <em>Inc</em>., 551 U.&nbsp;S., at 500-501 (opinion concurring in part).  William Gobitas,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 71" name="FRopinion1.71" href="#FNopinion1.71">71</a></sup> a 10-year-old fifth grader, and his 12-year-old sister Lillian refused to salute the flag during the Pledge of Allegiance because, along with other Jehovah's Witnesses, they thought the salute constituted idolatry.  310 U.&nbsp;S., at 591-592.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 72" name="FRopinion1.72" href="#FNopinion1.72">72</a></sup>  William's "teacher tried to force his arm up, but William held on to his pocket and successfully resisted."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 73" name="FRopinion1.73" href="#FNopinion1.73">73</a></sup>  The Gobitas children were expelled from school, and the family grocery was boycotted.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 74" name="FRopinion1.74" href="#FNopinion1.74">74</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This Court upheld the children's expulsion because, in ringing rhetoric quoted by <em>Smith</em>, "[c]onscientious scruples have not, in the course of the long struggle for religious toleration, relieved the individual from obedience to a general law not aimed at the promotion or restriction of religious beliefs."  310 U.&nbsp;S., at 594; see also <em>Smith</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S., at 879 (quoting this passage).  This declaration was overblown when issued in 1940.  (As noted, many religious exemptions had been granted by legislative bodies, and the 1940 statute instituting the peacetime draft continued that tradition by exempting conscientious objectors.  Selective Training and Service Act, 54 Stat. 885, 889.)  By 1990, when <em>Smith</em> was handed down, the pronouncement flew in the face of nearly 40 years of Supreme Court precedent.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But even if all that is put aside, <em>Smith</em>'s recourse to <em>Gobitis</em> was surprising because the decision was overruled just three years later when three of the Justices in the majority had second thoughts.  See <em>Barnette</em>, 319 U.&nbsp;S. 642; <em>id.,</em> at 643-644 (Black and Douglas, JJ., concurring); <em>id.,</em> at 644-646 (Murphy, J., concurring).  Turning <em>Gobitis</em>'s words on their head, <em>Barnette </em>held that students with religious objections to saluting the flag were indeed "relieved .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. from obedience to a general [rule] not aimed at the promotion or restriction of religious beliefs."  <em>Gobitis</em>, 310 U.&nbsp;S., at 594.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;After reviving <em>Gobitis'</em>s<em> </em>anti-exemption rhetoric, <em>Smith </em>turned to <em>Reynolds </em>v.<em> United States</em>, 98 U.&nbsp;S. 145, an 1879 decision upholding the polygamy conviction of a member of the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints.  Unlike <em>Gobitis</em>, <em>Reynolds</em> at least had not been overruled,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 75" name="FRopinion1.75" href="#FNopinion1.75">75</a></sup> but the decision was not based on anything like <em>Smith</em>'s interpretation of the Free Exercise Clause.  It rested primarily on the proposition that the Free Exercise Clause protects beliefs, not conduct.  98 U.&nbsp;S., at 166-167.  The Court had repudiated that distinction a half century before <em>Smith</em> was decided.  See <em>Cantwell</em>, 310 U.&nbsp;S., at 303-304; <em>Murdock </em>v.<em> Pennsylvania</em>, 319 U.&nbsp;S. 105, 110-111, 117 (1943).  And <em>Smith</em> itself agreed!  See 494 U.&nbsp;S., at 877.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The remaining pre-<em>Sherbert</em> cases cited by <em>Smith </em>actually cut against its interpretation.  None was based on the rule that <em>Smith</em> adopted.  Although these decisions ended up denying exemptions, they did so on other grounds.  In <em>Prince </em>v.<em> Massachusetts</em>, 321 U.&nbsp;S. 158 (1944), where a Jehovah's Witness who enlisted a child to distribute religious literature was convicted for violating a state child labor law, the decision was based on the Court's assessment of the strength of the State's interest.  <em>Id</em>., at 159-160, 162, 169-170; see also <em>Yoder</em>, 406 U.&nbsp;S., at 230-231 (describing the <em>Prince</em> Court's rationale).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In <em>Braunfeld </em>v.<em> Brown</em>, 366 U.&nbsp;S. 599, 601, 609 (1961) (plurality opinion), which rejected a Jewish merchant's challenge to Pennsylvania's Sunday closing laws, the Court balanced the competing interests.  The Court attached diminished weight to the burden imposed by the law (because it did not require work on Saturday), <em>id.,</em> at 606,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 76" name="FRopinion1.76" href="#FNopinion1.76">76</a></sup> and on the other side of the balance, the Court accepted the Commonwealth's view that the public welfare was served by providing a uniform day of rest, <em>id</em>., at 608-609; see <em>Sherbert</em>, 374 U.&nbsp;S., at 408-409 (discussing <em>Braunfeld</em>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;When <em>Smith</em> came to post-<em>Sherbert</em> cases, the picture did not improve.  First, in order to place <em>Sherbert</em>, <em>Hobbie</em>, and <em>Thomas</em> in a special category reserved for cases involving unemployment compensation, an inventive transformation was required.  None of those opinions contained a hint that they were limited in that way.  And since <em>Smith</em> itself involved the award of unemployment compensation benefits under a scheme that allowed individualized exemptions, it is hard to see why that case did not fall into the same category.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court<em> </em>tried to escape this problem by framing Alfred Smith's and Galen Black's free-exercise claims as requests for exemptions from the Oregon law criminalizing the possession of peyote, see 494 U.&nbsp;S., at 876, but neither Smith nor Black was prosecuted for that offense even though the State was well aware of what they had done.  The State had the discretion to decline prosecution based on the facts of particular cases, and that is presumably what it did regarding Smith and Black.  Why this was not sufficient to bring the case within <em>Smith</em>'s rule about individualized exemptions is unclear.  See McConnell, Free Exercise Revisionism 1124.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Having pigeon-holed <em>Sherbert</em>, <em>Hobbie</em>, and <em>Thomas</em> as unemployment compensation decisions, <em>Smith</em> still faced problems.  For one thing, the Court had previously applied the <em>Sherbert </em>test<em> </em>in many cases not involving unemployment compensation, including <em>Hernandez </em>v. <em>Commissioner</em>, 490 U.&nbsp;S. 680 (1989) (disallowance of tax deduction); <em>Lee</em>, 455 U.&nbsp;S. 252 (payment of taxes); and <em>Gillette, </em>401 U.&nbsp;S. 437 (denial of conscientious objector status to person with religious objection to a particular war).  To get these cases out of the way, <em>Smith </em>claimed that, because they ultimately found no free-exercise violations, they merely "<em>purported</em> to apply the <em>Sherbert</em> test."  494 U.&nbsp;S., at 883 (emphasis added).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This was a curious observation.  In all those cases, the Court invoked the <em>Sherbert </em>test but found that it did not require relief.  See <em>Hernandez</em>, 490 U.&nbsp;S., at 699; <em>Lee</em>, 455 U. S., at 257-260; <em>Gillette</em>, 401 U.&nbsp;S., at 462<em>.</em>  Was the <em>Smith</em> Court questioning the sincerity of these earlier opinions?  If not, then in what sense did those decisions merely "purport" to apply <em>Sherbert</em>?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Finally, having swept all these cases from the board, <em>Smith </em>still faced at least one big troublesome precedent: <em>Yoder</em>.  <em>Yoder</em> not only applied the <em>Sherbert</em> test but held that the Free Exercise Clause required an exemption totally unrelated to unemployment benefits.  406 U.&nbsp;S., at 220-221, 236.  To dispose of <em>Yoder</em>, <em>Smith</em> was forced to invent yet another special category of cases, those involving "hybrid-rights" claims.  <em>Yoder</em> fell into this category because it implicated both the Amish parents' free-exercise claim and a parental-rights claim stemming from <em>Pierce </em>v<em>. Society of Sisters</em>, 268 U.&nbsp;S. 510 (1925).  See <em>Smith</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S., at 881.  And in such hybrid cases, <em>Smith</em> held, the <em>Sherbert</em> test survived.  See<em> </em>494 U.&nbsp;S., at 881-882.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is hard to see the justification for this curious doctrine.  The idea seems to be that if two independently insufficient constitutional claims join forces they may merge into a single valid hybrid claim, but surely the rule cannot be that asserting two invalid claims, no matter how weak, is always enough.  So perhaps the doctrine requires the assignment of a numerical score to each claim.  If a passing grade is 70 and a party advances a free-speech claim that earns a grade of 40 and a free-exercise claim that merits a grade of 31, the result would be a (barely) sufficient hybrid claim.  Such a scheme is obviously unworkable and has never been recognized outside of <em>Smith</em>.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;And then there is the problem that the hybrid-rights exception would largely swallow up <em>Smith</em>'s general rule.  A great many claims for religious exemptions can easily be understood as hybrid free-exercise/free-speech claims.  Take the claim in <em>Smith</em> itself.  To members of the Native American Church, the ingestion of peyote during a religious ceremony is a sacrament.  When Smith and Black participated in this sacrament, weren't they engaging in a form of expressive conduct?  Their ingestion of peyote "communicate[d], in a rather dramatic way, [their] faith in the tenets of the Native American Church," and the State's prohibition of that practice "interfered with their ability to communicate this message" in violation of the Free Speech Clause.  McConnell, Free Exercise Revisionism 1122.  And, "if a hybrid claim is one in which a litigant would actually obtain an exemption from a formally neutral, generally applicable law under <em>another </em>constitutional provision, then there would have been no reason for the Court in [the so-called] hybrid cases to have mentioned the Free Exercise Clause at all."  <em>Lukumi</em>, 508 U.&nbsp;S., at 566-567 (opinion of Souter, J.); see also Laycock, 8 J. L. &amp; Religion, at 106 (noting that <em>Smith</em> "reduces the free exercise clause to a cautious redundancy, relevant only to 'hybrid' cases").  It is telling that this Court has never once accepted a "hybrid rights" claim in the more than three decades since <em>Smith</em>.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In addition to all these maneuvers--creating special categories for unemployment compensation cases, cases involving individualized exemptions, and hybrid-rights cases--<em>Smith</em> ignored the multiple occasions when the Court had directly repudiated the very rule that <em>Smith </em>adopted.  See <em>supra,</em> at 13-14.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Smith</em>'s rough treatment of prior decisions diminishes its own status as a precedent.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <em>Consistency with other precedents</em>.<em>  Smith</em> is also discordant with other precedents.  <em>Smith</em> did not overrule <em>Sherbert</em> or any of the other cases that built on <em>Sherbert </em>from 1963 to 1990, and for the reasons just discussed, <em>Smith </em>is tough to harmonize with those precedents.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The same is true about more recent decisions.  In <em>Hosanna-Tabor Evangelical Lutheran Church and School </em>v<em>. EEOC</em>, 565 U.&nbsp;S. 171 (2012), the Court essentially held that the First Amendment entitled a religious school to a special exemption from the requirements of the Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990 (ADA), 104 Stat. 327, 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 12101 <em>et seq</em>.  When the school discharged a teacher, she claimed that she had been terminated because of disability.  565 U.&nbsp;S., at 178-179.  Since the school considered her a "minister" and she provided religious instruction for her students, the school argued that her discharge fell within the so-called "ministerial exception" to generally applicable employment laws.  <em>Id.,</em> at 180.  The Equal Employment Opportunity Commission maintained that <em>Smith</em> precluded recognition of this exception because "the ADA's prohibition on retaliation, like Oregon's prohibition on peyote use, is a valid and neutral law of general applicability."  <em>Id.</em>, at 190; see <em>id.</em>, at 189-190.  We nevertheless held that the exception applied.  <em>Id.,</em> at 190.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 77" name="FRopinion1.77" href="#FNopinion1.77">77</a></sup>  Similarly, in <em>Our Lady of Guadalupe School </em>v.<em> Morrissey-Berru</em>, 591 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___-___ (2020) (slip op., at 21-22), we found that other religious schools were entitled to similar exemptions from both the ADA and the Age Discrimination in Employment Act of 1967.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;There is also tension between <em>Smith</em> and our opinion in <em>Masterpiece Cakeshop, Ltd. </em>v<em>. Colorado Civil Rights Comm'n, </em>584 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2018).  In that case, we observed that "[w]hen it comes to weddings, it can be assumed that a member of the clergy who objects to gay marriage on moral and religious grounds could not be compelled to perform the ceremony without denial of his or her right to the free exercise of religion."  <em>Id</em>., at ___ (slip op., at 10).  The clear import of this observation is that such a member of the clergy would be entitled to a religious exemption from a state law restricting the authority to perform a state-recognized marriage to individuals who are willing to officiate both opposite-sex and same-sex weddings.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Other inconsistencies exist.  <em>Smith</em> declared that "a private right to ignore generally applicable laws" would be a "constitutional anomaly," 494 U.&nbsp;S., at 886, but this Court has often permitted exemptions from generally applicable laws in First Amendment cases.  For instance, in <em>Boy Scouts of America </em>v.<em> Dale</em>, 530 U. S. 640, 656 (2000), we granted the Boy Scouts an exemption from an otherwise generally applicable state public accommodations law.  In <em>Hurley </em>v<em>. Irish-American Gay, Lesbian and Bisexual Group of Boston</em>, <em>Inc</em>., 515 U.&nbsp;S. 557, 573 (1995), parade sponsors' speech was exempted from the requirements of a similar law.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The granting of an exemption from a generally applicable law is tantamount to a holding that a law is unconstitutional as applied to a particular set of facts, see Barclay &amp; Rienzi, Constitutional Anomalies or As-Applied Challenges? A Defense of Religious&nbsp;Exemptions, 59 Boston College L. Rev. 1595, 1611 (2018), and cases holding generally applicable laws unconstitutional as applied are unremarkable.  "[T]he normal rule is that partial, rather than facial, invalidation is the required course, such that a statute may .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. be declared invalid to the extent that it reaches too far, but otherwise left intact."  Ayotte v. Planned Parenthood of Northern New Eng., 546 U.&nbsp;S. 320, 329 (2006) (internal quotation marks omitted; emphasis added).  Thus, in <em>Brown</em> v. <em>Socialist Workers '74 Campaign Comm. (Ohio)</em>, 459 U.&nbsp;S. 87 (1982), we held that a law requiring disclosure of campaign contributions and expenditures could not be "constitutionally applied" to a minor party whose members and contributors would face "threats, harassment or reprisals." <em>Id.</em>, at 101-102.  Cf. <em>NAACP </em>v<em>. Alabama ex&nbsp;rel. Patterson</em>, 357 U.&nbsp;S. 449, 466 (1958) (exempting the NAACP from a disclosure order entered to purportedly investigate compliance with a generally applicable statute).  In <em>Hustler Magazine, Inc. </em>v.<em> Falwell</em>, 485 U.&nbsp;S. 46, 56 (1988), and <em>Snyder </em>v<em>. Phelps</em>, 562 U.&nbsp;S. 443, 459 (2011), the Court held that an established and generally applicable tort claim (the intentional infliction of emotional distress) could not constitutionally be applied to the particular expression at issue.  Similarly, breach-of-the-peace laws, although generally valid, have been held to violate the Free Speech Clause under certain circumstances.  See <em>Cohen </em>v<em>. California</em>, 403 U.&nbsp;S. 15, 16, 26 (1971); <em>Cantwell</em>, 310 U.&nbsp;S., at 300, 311; see also <em>Bartnicki</em> v. <em>Vopper</em>, 532 U.&nbsp;S. 514, 517, 535 (2001) (respondents not liable under law prohibiting disclosure of illegally intercepted communications because their speech was protected by the First Amendment); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Treasury Employees</em>, 513 U.&nbsp;S. 454, 477 (1995) (respondents not subject to the honoraria ban because it would violate their First Amendment rights); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Grace</em>, 461 U.&nbsp;S. 171, 175, 179, 183 (1983) (respondents engaging in expressive conduct on public sidewalks not subject to law generally regulating conduct on Supreme Court grounds).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Finally, <em>Smith</em>'s treatment of the free-exercise right is fundamentally at odds with how we usually think about liberties guaranteed by the Bill of Rights.  As Justice Jackson famously put it, "[t]he very purpose of a Bill of Rights was to withdraw certain subjects from the vicissitudes of political controversy, to place them beyond the reach of majorities and officials."  <em>Barnette</em>, 319 U.&nbsp;S., at 638.  <em>Smith</em>, by contrast, held that protection of religious liberty was better left to the political process than to courts.  494 U.&nbsp;S., at 890.  In <em>Smith</em>'s view, the Nation simply could not "afford the luxury" of protecting the free exercise of religion from generally applicable laws.  <em>Id.</em>, at 888.  Under this interpretation, the free exercise of religion does not receive the judicial protection afforded to other, favored rights.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Workability</em>.  One of <em>Smith</em>'s supposed virtues was ease of application, but things have not turned out that way.  Instead, at least four serious problems have arisen and continue to plague courts when called upon to apply <em>Smith</em>.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Hybrid-rights" cases.  The "hybrid rights" exception, which was essential to distinguish <em>Yoder</em>, has baffled the lower courts.  They are divided into at least three camps.  See <em>Combs </em>v. <em>Homer-Center School Dist.</em>, 540 F.&nbsp;3d 231, 244-247 (CA3 2008) (describing Circuit split).  Some courts have taken the extraordinary step of openly refusing to follow this part of <em>Smith</em>'s interpretation.  The Sixth Circuit was remarkably blunt: "[H]old[ing] that the legal standard under the Free Exercise Clause depends on whether a free-exercise claim is coupled with other constitutional rights .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. is completely illogical."  <em>Kissinger </em>v<em>. Board of Trustees of Ohio State Univ.</em>, 5 F. 3d 177, 180 (1993).  The Second and Third Circuits have taken a similar approach.  See <em>Leebaert </em>v.<em> Harrington</em>, 332 F.&nbsp;3d 134, 144 (CA2 2003) ("We .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. can think of no good reason for the standard of review to vary simply with the number of constitutional rights that the plaintiff asserts have been violated"); <em>Knight </em>v<em>. Connecticut Dept</em>.<em> of Pub. Health</em>, 275 F. 3d 156, 167 (CA2 2001); <em>Combs</em>,<em> </em>540 F.&nbsp;3d, at 247 ("Until the Supreme Court provides direction, we believe the hybrid-rights theory to be dicta").</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;A second camp holds that the hybrid-rights exception applies only when a free-exercise claim is joined with some other independently viable claim.  See <em>Archdiocese of Washington </em>v. <em>WMATA</em>, 897 F.&nbsp;3d 314, 331 (CADC 2018) (A "hybrid rights claim .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. requires independently viable free speech and free exercise claims");<em> Gary S. </em>v. <em>Manchester School Dist.</em>, 374 F.&nbsp;3d 15, 19 (CA1 2004) (adopting District Court's reasoning that "the [hybrid-rights] exception can be invoked only if the plaintiff has joined a free exercise challenge with another independently viable constitutional claim," 241 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d 111, 121 (NH 2003));<em> Brown </em>v. <em>Hot, Sexy and Safer Productions</em>, 68 F.&nbsp;3d 525, 539 (CA1 1995).  But this approach essentially makes the free-exercise claim irrelevant.  See <em>Axson-Flynn </em>v. <em>Johnson</em>, 356 F.&nbsp;3d 1277, 1296-1297 (CA10 2004) ("[I]t makes no sense to adopt a strict standard that essentially requires a <em>successful</em> companion claim because such a test would make the free exercise claim unnecessary"); see also <em>Lukumi</em>, 508 U.&nbsp;S., at 567 (opinion of Souter, J.) (making the same point).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The third group requires that the non-free-exercise claim be "colorable."  See <em>Cornerstone Christian Schools </em>v. <em>University Interscholastic League</em>, 563 F.&nbsp;3d 127, 136, n.&nbsp;8 (CA5 2009);<em> San Jose Christian College </em>v. <em>Morgan Hill</em>, 360 F.&nbsp;3d 1024, 1032-1033 (CA9 2004); <em>Axson-Flynn, </em>356 F.&nbsp;3d, at 1295-1297.  But what that means is obscure.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>id</em>., at 1295 (referring to "helpful" analogies such as the "&nbsp;'likelihood of success on the merits' standard for preliminary injunctions" or the pre-Antiterrorism and Effective Death Penalty Act standard for obtaining an evidentiary hearing, <em>i.e.</em>, a "&nbsp;'colorable showing of factual innocence'&nbsp;").<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 78" name="FRopinion1.78" href="#FNopinion1.78">78</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is rare to encounter a holding of this Court that has so thoroughly stymied or elicited such open derision from the Courts of Appeals.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Rules that "target" religion.  Post-<em>Smith</em> cases have also struggled with the task of determining whether a purportedly neutral rule "targets" religious exercise or has the restriction of religious exercise as its "object."  <em>Lukumi</em>, 508 U.&nbsp;S., at 534; <em>Smith</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S., at 878.  A threshold question is whether "targeting" calls for an objective or subjective inquiry.  Must "targeting" be assessed based solely on the terms of the relevant rule or rules?  Or can evidence of the rulemakers' motivation be taken into account?  If subjective motivations may be considered, does it matter whether the challenged state action is an adjudication, the promulgation of a rule, or the enactment of legislation?  Should courts consider the motivations of only the officials who took the challenged action, or may they also take into account comments by superiors and others in a position of influence?  And what degree of hostility to religion or a religious group is required to prove "targeting"?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The genesis of this problem was <em>Smith</em>'s holding that a rule is not neutral "if prohibiting the exercise of religion" is its "object."  494 U.&nbsp;S., at 878.  <em>Smith</em> did not elaborate on what that meant, and later in <em>Lukumi</em>, which concerned city ordinances that burdened the practice of Santeria, 508 U.&nbsp;S., at 525-528, Justices in the <em>Smith</em> majority adopted different interpretations.  Justice Scalia and Chief Justice Rehnquist took the position that the "object" of a rule must be determined by its terms and that evidence of the rulemakers' motivation should not be considered.  508 U.&nbsp;S., at 557-559.
